Classification of Ghusl: Fard, Sunnah, and Mandub
Lists the six obligatory types and the recommended types of ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six obligatory types of ghusl and distinguish them from mandub and sunnah types, and explain why some Hanafi scholars reject calling ghusl of the deceased or a convert 'wajib' instead of 'fard'.

Core Idea
Ghusl is fard for six specific causes; calling any of them 'wajib' risks confusion with Hanafi terminology.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq concludes the section on ghusl by providing a clear taxonomy. You will learn the six obligatory (fard) triggers, the four sunnah occasions (which are known from earlier material), and the recommended (mandub) scenarios. The author also addresses a terminological dispute: some scholars used 'wajib' for ghusl of the deceased and for a convert who was junub, but the commentator insists these are fard, not wajib, to avoid undermining the well-known Hanafi distinction between fard (definitive) and wajib (speculative).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
The passage divides ghusl into fard (obligatory), sunnah, and mandub (recommended). First, grasp the overall structure: six fard types, four sunnah types (not listed here but previously), and several mandub types.
The sunnah types are not repeated here; refer to earlier lessons for Friday, Eid, Ihram, etc.
2
List the six fard types
Memorize the six causes that make ghusl obligatory: (1) ejaculation with desire, (2) intercourse (even if the person was a disbeliever and later converts), (3) cessation of menstruation, (4) cessation of postpartum bleeding, (5) washing a deceased Muslim, (6) when an impurity covers the body or a hidden part.
The fifth is fard kifayah (communal obligation); the others are fard 'ayn (individual).
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi terminological dispute
Some earlier Hanafi scholars classified ghusl of the deceased and ghusl of a convert junub as 'wajib' (a level below fard). The commentator rejects this because these actions are truly fard, and calling them wajib may create confusion—since in Hanafi usul, wajib is proven by zanni evidence and fard by qat'i. The transmitted ruling (manqul) confirms they are fard.
The commentator's phrase 'it may be misunderstood as something other than fard' highlights the importance of precise terminology.
4
Examine the mandub types
The passage lists several recommended ghusls: (1) a disbeliever who converts but is not junub, (2) for entering Makkah, (3) for standing at Muzdalifah, (4) for entering Madinah, (5) for a person regaining sanity, (6) for a boy reaching puberty by age, (7) for one who has washed a deceased, (8) for cupping (due to a difference of opinion), and (9) on Laylat al-Qadr if one sees it.
These are not obligatory but carry reward; leaving them incurs no sin.
5
Apply the classification
Given a scenario (e.g., a woman who finishes her period, a man who wants to enter ihram, a person who had a wet dream), determine whether ghusl is fard, sunnah, mandub, or not required at all. This trains you to apply the taxonomy practically.
For the sixth fard type (impurity covering body), the key is that the location of najasah is hidden or extensive, otherwise local washing suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى هنا تمت أنواع الاغتسال وهي فرض وسنة ومندوب فالفرض ستة أنواع : من إنزال المني بشهوة، وتواري حشفة ولو كان كافراً ثم أسلم، ومن انقطاع حيض أو نفاس ولو كانت كافرة ثم أسلمت، والخامس غسل الميت، والسادس الغسل عند إصابة جميع بدنه نجاسة أو بعضه وخفي مكانها. وكثر من المشايخ قسموا أنواعه إلى فرض وواجب وسنة ومندوب وجعلوا الواجب غسل الميت وغسل الكافر إذا أسلم جنباً ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن هذا الذي سموه واجباً يفوت الجواز بفوته . والمنقول في باب الجنائز أن غسل الميت فرض فالأولى عدم إطلاق الواجب عليه لأنه ربما يتوهم أنه غير الفرض بناء على اصطلاحنا المشهور والمسنون أربعة كما تقدم، والمندوب غسل الكافر إذا أسلم غير جنب، ولدخول مكة والوقوف بمزدلفة ودخول مدينة النبي ، وللمجنون إذا أفاق، والصبي إذا بلغ بالسن ومن غسل الميت وللحجامة لشبهة الخلاف، وليلة القدر إذا رآها،
And up to here, the types of ghusl have been completed: they are fard, sunnah, and mandub. Fard is of six types: (1) from ejaculation of semen with desire, (2) from concealment of the glans (even if a disbeliever then becomes Muslim), (3) from cessation of menstruation or lochia (even if a disbelieving woman then becomes Muslim), (5) ghusl of the deceased, and (6) ghusl when all of his body or part of it is affected by impurity and the location is hidden. Many scholars divided its types into fard, wajib, sunnah, and mandub, and made the wajib: ghusl of the deceased and ghusl of a disbeliever who becomes Muslim while junub. And it is not hidden what is in this, for what they call wajib, permissibility is lost by its loss. The transmitted ruling in the chapter of funerals is that ghusl of the deceased is fard, so it is better not to apply the term wajib to it, because it may be misunderstood as something other than fard based on our well-known convention. The sunnah types are four as previously mentioned. The mandub (recommended) types are: ghusl of a disbeliever who becomes Muslim while not junub, for entering Mecca, for standing at Muzdalifah, for entering the city of the Prophet, for a person who regains sanity, for a boy who reaches puberty by age, for one who has washed the deceased, for cupping due to a semblance of difference, and for the Night of Qadr if he sees it.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage concludes the section on ghusl by enumerating its categories. The six obligatory (fard) causes are: 1) emission of semen with desire, 2) intercourse (hiding the glans), even if the person was a disbeliever who later converts (meaning ghusl becomes obligatory upon conversion if they had intercourse earlier), 3) cessation of menstruation, 4) cessation of lochia (postpartum bleeding), 5) washing a deceased Muslim, 6) when a person's entire body or a hidden part is touched by najasah (impurity) such that the location is unknown, requiring a full ghusl instead of just washing the affected part. Some scholars add a category of 'wajib' (which in Hanafi usage is a degree below fard) for ghusl of the deceased and ghusl of the convert who was junub, but the commentator objects because these are actually fard, and calling them wajib may cause confusion. The sunnah ghusls are four (previously mentioned: for Friday, Eid, entering ihram, etc.). The mandub (recommended) ghusls include: conversion without janabah, entering Makkah, standing at Muzdalifah, entering Madinah, regaining sanity, puberty by age, after washing a deceased, after cupping (because of some disagreement on its ruling), and on Laylat al-Qadr if one sees it (indicating it is special).

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Obligatory upon a Muslim, violation incurs sin unless omitted with excuse.
مندوب (mandub): Recommended act; reward for doing, no sin for leaving.
النجاسة (al-najāsah): Ritual impurity (physical), such as urine or blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of fard ghusl are listed in this passage?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Three

Key Takeaway
Ghusl is fard for six specific causes; calling any of them 'wajib' risks confusion with Hanafi terminology, so the mufta bihi is to label all six as fard.

Obligation of Ghusl in Conversion and Puberty
Resolves intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl for conversion and puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios for obligatory ghusl, identify the differing Hanafi views, and apply the mufta bihi position of Qadikhan.

Core Idea
Ghusl is wajib in all four conversion/puberty scenarios according to the mufta bihi view.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about when ghusl becomes obligatory upon conversion and puberty. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq navigates four specific scenarios—conversion while junub, conversion of a menstruating woman, and puberty for a boy and girl. By understanding the reasoning, you will be able to evaluate the weight of competing arguments and apply the cautionary position of Qadikhan, which is the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Scenarios
List the four cases under dispute: (1) a non-Muslim converts while junub, (2) a menstruating woman converts then becomes pure, (3) a boy reaches puberty by nocturnal emission, (4) a girl reaches puberty by menstruation.
These are called 'four chapters' in the text.
2
State the Disputed Rulings
For scenarios (3) and (4), some early Hanafis said ghusl is obligatory on the girl but not the boy. For (1) and (2), wujub was more widely accepted, but the objector differentiates between conversion while junub and conversion while menstruating after cessation.
The dispute hinges on the definition of the cause (sabab) for ghusl.
3
Analyze Qadikhan's Position
Qadikhan adopts the cautious view: wujub in all four chapters. His reasoning is based on analogy with the undisputed obligation of wudu for a convert with hadath, and on the idea that menstruation causes a hadath similar to janabah.
He cites Fath al-Qadir for the wudu analogy.
4
Examine the Counter-Arguments
The objector argues that the cause for ghusl after menstruation is the cessation of bleeding, which only occurs after puberty. For the boy, the cause is the nocturnal emission itself. The moment of puberty is like the beginning of capacity, not a direct cause. Also, some argue that the obligation of prayer, not cessation, is the real cause for a menstruating woman.
The verifier (al-Muhhaqqiq) could not fully answer the objection about the woman converting after cessation.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq prefers Qadikhan's view, supported by Miraj al-Dirayah and Amali Qadikhan, concluding that ghusl is obligatory in all cases. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on caution and the strength of the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة ۱۲۱ اهـ. الإسلام فكأنه أجنب بعده والانقطاع في الحيض هو السبب ولم يتحقق بعد فلذلك لو أسلمت حائضاً ثم طهرت وجب عليها الغسل. ولو بلغ الصبي بالاحتلام أو هي بالحيض قيل يجب عليها لا عليه فهذه أربعة فصول . قال قاضيخان والأحوط وجوب الغسل في الفصول كلها وفي فتح القدير : ولا نعلم خلافاً في وجوب الوضوء للصلاة إذا أسلم محدثاً، ولا معنى للفرق بين هاتين فإنه إن اعتبر حال البلوغ أوان انعقاد أهلية التكليف فهو كحال انعقاد العلة لا يجب عليهما وإن اعتبر أوان توجه الخطاب حتى ا اتحد زمانهما وجب عليهما، والحيض إما حدث أو يوجب حدثاً في رتبة حدث الجنابة كما سنحققه في بابه فوجب أن يتحد حكمه بالذي أسلم جنباً. وجوابه أن السبب في الحيض الانقطاع وثبوته بعد البلوغ لتحقق البلوغ بابتداء الحيض كيلا يثبت الانقطاع إلا وهي بالغة ا هـ . وهذا الجواب بعد تسليمه يصلح جواباً عما يرد على الفرق بين المرأة إذا بلغت بالحيض والصبي إذا بلغ بالاحتلام ولقائل أن يمنعه لما تقدم أن المختار أن السبب في وجوب الغسل على الحائض ليس الحيض ولا انقطاعه وإنما هو وجوب الصلاة، فحينئذ لا فرق بينهما والجواب الصحيح أن الصحيح وجوب الاغتسال على الصبي إذا بلغ بالاحتلام ذكره في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى أمالي قاضيخان وأما ما يرد على الفرق بين المرأة الحائض إذا أسلمت بعد الانقطاع وبين المسلم إذا كان جنباً فلم يحصل الجواب عنه من المحقق، فالأولى القول بالوجوب عليهما كما ذكره قاضيخان
The Book of Purity 121 end. Islam, so he is like an impure after it, and the cessation in menstruation is the cause and has not been realized yet, and therefore if a menstruating woman converted to Islam then became pure, ghusl is obligatory upon her. And if a boy reached puberty by nocturnal emission or she by menstruation, it was said: it is obligatory upon her, not upon him. These are four chapters. Qadikhan said: And the most cautious is the obligation of ghusl in all the chapters. And in Fath al-Qadir: And we know of no disagreement regarding the obligation of wudu for prayer if one converts as a hadath-holder, and there is no meaning for the distinction between these two. Because if the moment of puberty is considered the time of the inception of the capacity for obligation, it is like the inception of the cause, so it is not obligatory upon them. And if the moment of the address is considered even if the times are united, it is obligatory upon them. Menstruation is either a hadath or causes a hadath in the rank of the hadath of janabah as we will verify in its chapter, so it is obligatory that its ruling is united with the one who converts while junub. And his answer is that the cause in menstruation is cessation, and its establishment after puberty is for the verification of puberty by the beginning of menstruation so that cessation is not established except while she is pubescent. End. And this answer, after accepting it, is suitable as a response to what is objected regarding the distinction between a woman who reaches puberty by menstruation and a boy who reaches puberty by nocturnal emission. And someone may refute it because of what preceded that the chosen view is that the cause for obligation of ghusl upon the menstruating woman is not menstruation nor its cessation but rather the obligation of prayer. At that moment, there is no distinction between them. And the correct answer is that the correct view is the obligation of washing upon the boy who reaches puberty by nocturnal emission, mentioned in Miraj al-Dirayah attributed to the Amali of Qadikhan. As for what is objected regarding the distinction between a menstruating woman who converts after cessation and a Muslim who was junub, there is no answer obtained from the verifier. So the first is to say obligation upon both as Qadikhan mentioned.

Commentary
هذا النص هو مناقشة فقهية داخل المذهب الحنفي في وجوب الغسل في أربع مسائل: إسلام الكافر وهو جنب، إسلام الحائض ثم طهرها، بلوغ الصبي بالاحتلام، وبلوغ الفتاة بالحيض. يبدأ النص بتقرير أن سبب وجوب الغسل على الحائض هو الانقطاع (الطهر) وليس الحيض نفسه، فإذا أسلمت حائضاً ثم طهرت يجب عليها الغسل. ثم يذكر خلافاً في وجوب الغسل عند البلوغ: قيل يجب على الأنثى لا على الذكر. وينقل قول قاضيخان بالاحتياط بالوجوب في الجميع. ويستشهد بفتح القدير بأنه لا خلاف في وجوب الوضوء إذا أسلم محدثاً. ثم يسوق دليلاً عقلياً على وجوب الغسل في الحائض بعد طهرها: أن الحيض إما حدث أو يوجب حدثاً في مرتبة الجنابة، فيتحد حكمه مع من أسلم جنباً. ثم يرد على هذا الدليل بأن السبب هو الانقطاع بعد البلوغ. ثم يناقش الفرق بين البلوغ بالحيض والبلوغ بالاحتلام، ويخلص إلى أن الصحيح وجوب الغسل على الصبي بالاحتلام كما في معراج الدراية. وأخيراً يرجح قول قاضيخان بالوجوب على المرأة الحائض إذا أسلمت بعد الانقطاع وعلى المسلم الذي كان جنباً.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl
حائض (ha'id): A woman in the state of menstruation
احتلام (ihtilam): Nocturnal emission of semen
فصول (fusul): Chapters or scenarios in this context
انقطاع (inqita'): Cessation of menstrual bleeding

Types of Ghusl: Fard, Sunnah, and Mandub
Classifies ghusl into obligatory, sunnah, and recommended with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of ghusl (fard, sunnah, mandub), list their specific examples according to the Hanafi school, and explain why ghusl al-mayyit is fard and not wajib.

Core Idea
Ghusl is either fard (6 causes), sunnah (4 occasions), or mandub (many) — a precise classification defines obligation.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author completes the discussion on ghusl by categorizing it into three legal levels: fard, sunnah, and mandub. He provides explicit examples for each and responds to those who incorrectly label some obligatory types as merely wajib. This classification is foundational for practical taharah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories
Read the opening: the types are fard, sunnah, and mandub. Note that fard is subdivided into six, sunnah into four, and mandub includes many occasions.
2
Memorize the six fard types
List them: (1) discharge of semen with desire, (2) concealment of glans (intercourse), (3) cessation of menstruation, (4) cessation of postpartum bleeding, (5) washing the deceased, (6) washing when impurity covers the body in an unknown location.
The sixth type is specific to hidden impurity.
3
Recall the four sunnah types
These were mentioned earlier in the text: ghusl for Friday, the two Eid prayers, ihram, and standing at Arafat (ifafa). The author refers back to them.
Some later texts add more, but these four are the standard.
4
Recognize the mandub types
The author lists multiple: ghusl for a disbeliever converting while not junub, entering Mecca, standing at Muzdalifah, entering Madinah, for an insane person when regaining sanity, for a boy reaching puberty, for one who washed a deceased, for cupping (due to difference of opinion), and for Laylat al-Qadr if one sees it.
This list is illustrative, not exhaustive.
5
Understand the intra-Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib
Some earlier Hanafi scholars called ghusl al-mayyit and ghusl of a converting disbeliever wajib. The author argues they are fard, because their omission invalidates the act, and the established ruling is fard. This shows the importance of precise terminology.
Fard: based on qat'i evidence; wajib: based on dhanni evidence. Here, the evidence for ghusl al-mayyit is considered qat'i (ijma' like).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى هنا تمت أنواع الاغتسال وهي فرض وسنة ومندوب فالفرض ستة أنواع : من إنزال المني بشهوة، وتواري حشفة ولو كان كافراً ثم أسلم، ومن انقطاع حيض أو نفاس ولو كانت كافرة ثم أسلمت، والخامس غسل الميت، والسادس الغسل عند إصابة جميع بدنه نجاسة أو بعضه وخفي مكانها. وكثر من المشايخ قسموا أنواعه إلى فرض وواجب وسنة ومندوب وجعلوا الواجب غسل الميت وغسل الكافر إذا أسلم جنباً ولا يخفى ما فيه فإن هذا الذي سموه واجباً يفوت الجواز بفوته . والمنقول في باب الجنائز أن غسل الميت فرض فالأولى عدم إطلاق الواجب عليه لأنه ربما يتوهم أنه غير الفرض بناء على اصطلاحنا المشهور والمسنون أربعة كما تقدم، والمندوب غسل الكافر إذا أسلم غير جنب، ولدخول مكة والوقوف بمزدلفة ودخول مدينة النبي ، وللمجنون إذا أفاق، والصبي إذا بلغ بالسن ومن غسل الميت وللحجامة لشبهة الخلاف، وليلة القدر إذا رآها، . أجزأهم ذلك اهـ . قال : واختاره في الغاية والاسبيجابي لأن غسل الحي لا يشترط له النية فكذا غسل الميت قوله : (بزوالها) متعلق بالمشروط . وقوله إلا به أي بالغسل قوله : (ولو أجده لأئمتنا فيما عندي) قال في النهر صرح في الدرر والغرر بندب غسل الكسوف والاستسقاء اهـ. قلت: ومثله الشرنبلالي في متنه ثم رأيته أيضاً في شرح درر البحار التصريح الجمار، ثم رأيت في برمي معراج الدراية قيل يستحب الاغتسال لصلاة الكسوف وفي الاستسقاء وفي كل ما كان في معنى ذلك
And up to here the types of ghusl have been completed, and they are fard, sunnah, and mandub. The fard are six types: from the discharge of semen with desire; the concealment of the glans even if he was a disbeliever then converted; from the cessation of menstruation or postpartum bleeding even if she was a disbeliever then converted; the fifth is washing the deceased; the sixth is ghusl when all of his body or part of it is affected by impurity and its place is hidden. And many of the mashayikh divided its types into fard, wajib, sunnah, and mandub, and made the wajib: washing the deceased and washing the disbeliever when he converts while junub. And it is not hidden what is in this, because that which they called wajib, validity is lost by its loss. And what is transmitted in the chapter of funerals is that washing the deceased is fard, so it is better not to apply the term 'wajib' to it, because perhaps it might be understood that it is not fard based on our well-known convention. And the sunnah are four as mentioned previously. And the mandub is: ghusl of the disbeliever when he converts while not junub; for entering Mecca, standing at Muzdalifah, and entering the city of the Prophet (ﷺ); for the insane person when he recovers; the boy when he reaches puberty by age; and for the one who washes the deceased; and for cupping due to the semblance of disagreement; and for Laylat al-Qadr if he sees it. ... That suffices them. End. He said: And he chose it in al-Ghayah and al-Ispijabi because ghusl of the living does not require intention, so similarly ghusl of the deceased. His statement (upon its removal) relates to the conditional. And his statement 'except by it' means by ghusl. His statement (and I did not find it for our imams in what I have). He said in al-Nahr: 'It was stated explicitly in al-Durar wa al-Ghurar that ghusl for eclipse and rain prayer is mandub.' End. I say: And similarly al-Shurunbulali in his matn. Then I also saw in Sharh Durar al-Bihar the explicit mention of stoning the Jamarat. Then I saw in Miraj al-Dirayah: 'It is reported that ghusl is recommended for eclipse prayer, rain prayer, and everything similar.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
هذا النص هو تقسيم أنواع الغسل عند الحنفية إلى ثلاثة أقسام: فرض، وسنة، ومندوب. الفرض ستة: خروج المني بشهوة، والجماع، وانقطاع الحيض والنفاس، وغسل الميت، والغسل لإصابة نجاسة خفية. وينتقد المؤلف من جعل بعض هذه واجباً بدلاً من فرض، ويؤكد أن غسل الميت فرض عند الحنفية. والمسنون أربعة: غسل الجمعة، والعيدين، والإحرام، والإفاضة (وهذه ذكرت سابقاً). والمندوبات كثيرة منها: غسل الكافر إذا أسلم غير جنب، وغسل لدخول مكة ومزدلفة ومدينة النبي ﷺ، وللمجنون إذا أفاق، وللصبي إذا بلغ بالسن، ولمن غسل ميتاً، وللحجامة، وليلة القدر إذا رآها. ثم يضيف النص بعض التعليقات حول غسل الكسوف والاستسقاء ورمي الجمار وهذه كلها مندوبة.

Key Terms
غسل الميت (ghusl al-mayyit): تطهير الميت المسلم بالماء على صفة مخصوصة
واجب (wajib): ما يثاب فاعله ويسأل تاركه عتاباً لا عقاباً
مندوب (mandub): ما يثاب فاعله ولا إثم على تاركه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies ghusl into six fard, four sunnah, and numerous mandub occasions, with ghusl al-mayyit being fard not wajib.

Permissible Water for Wudu and the Definition of Mutlaq Water
Declares permissibility of using rain, spring, and sea water for wudu and defines mutlaq water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which waters are valid for wudu according to the Hanafi school and define mutlaq water, distinguishing it from muqayyad, impure, and used water.

Core Idea
All natural water — rain, spring, sea — is pure for wudu; the key is it must be 'mutlaq', not mixed with other substances.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the fundamental ruling on water for purification. It declares that water from the sky, springs, and seas is valid for wudu even if one of its properties has changed due to a pure substance. The author then defines the concept of mutlaq (absolute) water, which is the basis for validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on water types
Locate the sentence that lists the three types of water and the condition about a change in property. Note that the passage says wudu is valid even if one of its properties is changed by a pure substance.
The phrase 'وإن غير طاهر أحد أوصافه' indicates change by a pure substance does not invalidate.
2
Analyze the definition of mutlaq water
Focus on the definition: 'فالمطلق هو ما يسبق إلى الأفهام بمطلق قولنا ماء ولم يقم به خبث ولا معنى يمنع جواز الصلاة'. Understand that it means water that comes to mind when we say 'water' absolutely, and is free from impurity and anything that would prevent prayer.
The definition excludes three categories: muqayyad, najis, and musta'mal water.
3
Clarify the term 'jawaz'
The commentator mentions that the intended meaning of 'jawaz' in this context is validity (sihha), not permissibility (ibaha). This means the wudu is valid even if the water is not legally permissible to use (e.g., stolen water), though it would be sinful.
Shaykh Ismail's commentary emphasizes looking at validity apart from permissibility.
4
Apply the definition
Test your understanding by comparing mutlaq water with examples: rain water (mutlaq), rose water (muqayyad), water with a drop of urine (najis), and water already used for wudu (musta'mal). Confirm which are valid for wudu.
The passage explicitly excludes the last three from the definition of mutlaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسلم جنباً وإلا ندب ويتوضأ بماء السماء والعين والبحر وإن غير طاهر أحد أوصافه. واعلم أن الماء نوعان مطلق ومقيد. فالمطلق هو ما يسبق إلى الأفهام بمطلق قولنا ماء ولم يقم به خبث ولا معنى يمنع جواز الصلاة فخرج الماء المقيد والماء المتنجس والماء المستعمل. والمطلق في الأصول هو المتعرض للذات دون الصفات لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات. والمراد هنا (الأول) أي الحل لأن الطهارة تكون بما هو من الأفعال كالوضوء ونحوه. وفي شرح الشيخ إسمعيل : الظاهر هنا الصحة مع قطع النظر عن الحل وعدمه.
He converts while junub, otherwise it is recommended. And he performs wudu with water of the sky, spring, and sea, even if one of its descriptions is changed. And know that water is of two types: absolute and restricted. The absolute is what comes to mind when we say 'water' absolutely, and neither impurity nor a meaning that prevents permissibility of prayer is attached to it. So restricted water, impure water, and used water are excluded. And absolute in usul is that which concerns the essence without attributes, neither by negation nor affirmation. And what is intended here is the first (i.e., permissibility), because purification is of the type of acts like wudu and similar. And in the commentary of Shaykh Ismail: The apparent here is validity without regard to permissibility or lack thereof.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
هذا النص يقرر حكم الماء الذي يجوز به الوضوء، وهو ماء السماء (المطر) والعيون والبحار، حتى لو تغير أحد أوصافه (لون أو طعم أو ريح) بطاهر. ثم يعرف الماء المطلق بأنه ما يتبادر إلى الذهن عند إطلاق لفظ 'ماء' بغير قيد، ولم يخالطه نجس أو غيره مما يمنع صحة الصلاة. ويخرج بقية الأنواع كالماء المقيد (كماء الورد) والمتنجس والمستعمل. وينقل عن شرح الشيخ إسمعيل أن المراد بالجواز هنا هو الصحة (أي يصح الوضوء به) وإن لم يكن مباحاً (مثل الماء المغصوب). كما يرد النص على من قال إن ماء البحر ليس بماء طهور، بإثبات أنه ماء مطلق.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): الماء الذي يبقى على أصل خلقته ولم يغلب عليه غيره من الطاهرات
ماء مقيد (ma' muqayyad): الماء الذي أضيف إلى شيء كالورد أو العصير
جواز (jawaz): هنا بمعنى صحة الفعل، لا حل الفعل (كما في شرح الشيخ إسمعيل)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
ماذا يعني الماء المطلق عند الحنفية بحسب التعريف في النص؟
A) الماء الذي لم يتغير أحد أوصافه بنجاسة
B) الماء الذي يخطر بالبال عند إطلاق كلمة ماء
C) الماء المستعمل في رفع الحدث
D) ماء البحر

Key Takeaway
All natural water that is absolutely called 'water' (mutlaq) is valid for wudu, regardless of minor changes by pure substances; water that is mixed, impure, or previously used is not.

6 min
Ruling on Wudu with Natural Waters: Rain, Springs, and Sea
Commentary on the permissibility of natural waters for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu with rainwater, spring water, and seawater, and explain why these waters are considered pure (mutlaq) for purification.

Core Idea
Rain, spring, and seawater are all valid for wudu — natural water is pure unless altered by impurity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a classic Hanafi ruling: wudu is valid with rainwater, spring water, and seawater. The commentary goes beyond the ruling to clarify key terms like 'jawaz' (permissibility vs. validity) and 'al-ayn' (spring). It also defends the inclusion of seawater against a minority view and defines the concept of mutlaq water. Understanding this ruling reinforces the Hanafi principle that all natural waters are inherently pure and purifying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states one may perform wudu with three types of water. Identify the hukm: it is permissible/valid.
The ruling is clear but the commentary expands on the meaning of 'jawaz'.
2
Clarify the term 'jawaz'
The commentator explains that 'jawaz' can mean permissibility (halal) or validity (sihhah). Here it primarily means permissibility for actions like wudu.
Validity is entailed by permissibility but not vice versa.
3
Examine the definition of 'al-ayn'
'Al-ayn' is a homonym with many meanings. The context of water types indicates it means 'spring' (yambu').
Context (qarinah) disambiguates homonyms in legal texts.
4
Understand the classification of water
Water is divided into mutlaq (absolute) and muqayyad (restricted). Mutlaq water is the default for purification.
The passage defines mutlaq as what comes to mind when we say 'water' absolutely, free from impurity or preventing conditions.

قوله: (ويتوضأ بماء السماء والعين والبحر) يعني الطهارة جائزة بماء السماء كما صرح به القدوري وغيره والمشايخ تارة يطلقون الجواز بمعنى الحل، وتارة بمعنى الصحة وهي لازمة للأول من غير عكس والغالب إرادة الأول في الأفعال، والثاني في العقود والمراد هنا الأول، قال ومن بعموم المشترك استعمل الجواز هنا بالمعنيين. والماء هو الجسم اللطيف السيال الذي به حياة كل نام وأصله موه بالتحريك وهو أصل مرفوض فيما أبدل من الهاء إبدالاً لازماً فإن الهمزة فيه مبدلة عن الهاء في موضع اللام ويجمع على مياه جمع كثرة، وجمع قلة على أمواه والعين لفظ مشترك بين الشمس والينبوع والذهب والدينار والمال والنقد والجاسوس والمطر وولد البقر الوحشي وخيار الشيء ونفس الشيء والناس القليل وحرف من حروف المعجم وما عن يمين قبلة العراق وعين في الجلد وغير ذلك، والمراد به هنا الينبوع بقرينة السياق وفي قوله والبحر عطفاً على السماء أي وبماء البحر إشارة إلى ردّ قول من قال إن ماء البحر ليس بماء حتى حكي عن ابن عمران أنه قال في ماء البحر : التيمم أحب إلي منه كما نقله عنه في السراج الوهاج وقسم هذه المياه باعتبار ما يشاهد عادة وإلا فالكل من السماء لقوله تعالى ألم تر أن الله أنزل من السماء ماء فسلكه ينابيع في الأرض [الزمر: ۲۱] وقيل ليس في الآية أن جميع المياه تنزل من السماء لأن «ما» نكرة في الإثبات ومعلوم أنها لا تعم قلنا بل تعم بقرينة الامتنان به فإن الله ذكره في معرض الامتنان به فلو لم يدل على العموم لفات المطلوب، والنكرة في الإثبات تفيد العموم بقرينة تدل عليه كما في قوله تعالى علمت نفس ما أحضرت [التكوير : ١٤] أي كل نفس. واعلم أن الماء نوعان مطلق ومقيد. فالمطلق هو ما يسبق إلى الأفهام بمطلق قولنا ماء ولم يقم به خبث ولا معنى يمنع جواز الصلاة فخرج الماء المقيد والماء المتنجس والماء المستعمل والمطلق في الأصول هو المتعرض للذات دون الصفات لا بالنفي ولا باجتماع الناس قوله : (والمراد هنا (الأول) أي الحل لأن الطهارة تكون بما هو من الأفعال كالوضوء ونحوه. وفي شرح الشيخ إسمعيل : الظاهر هنا الصحة مع قطع النظر عن الحل وعدمه. (ومن قال بعموم المشترك استعمل الجواز هنا بالمعنيين) أقول: أما وجه استعماله بمعنى الحل فلما تقدم، وأما وجه استعماله بمعنى الصحة فلأنها لازمة للحل من غير عكس وهنا كذلك فإن الطهارة قد تصح وتحل وقد تصح ولا تحل كالطهارة بماء مباح أو بماء لغير قوله : (والمراد هنا الينبوع بقرينة السياق قال في النهر : هذا مبني على أنه معطوف على ماء وبعده لا يخفى والأولى أن يعطف على السماء وعليه فلا يكون مشتركاً بين ما ذكر نعم هو مشترك بينه وبين ماء الباصرة والثاني غير مراد بقرينة السياق .اهـ. ويمكن تقدير مضاف في كلام الشارح أي ماء الينبوع فيؤول إلى ما ذكر.
His statement: (And one performs wudu with rainwater, spring water, and seawater) means purification is permitted with rainwater as stated by al-Quduri and others. The scholars sometimes use 'permissibility' (jawaz) in the sense of legal permissibility (halal), and sometimes in the sense of validity (sihhah). Validity is necessarily entailed by permissibility but not vice versa. The general usage in actions is the former, and in contracts the latter. The intended meaning here is the former. Some say, based on the general application of the homonym, that 'jawaz' is used here in both senses. Water is the subtle flowing body by which every living thing lives. Its root is 'mawah' with vowel movement, and it is a discarded root as the hamza is a permanent substitution for the ha' in the position of the lam. It is pluralized as 'miyah' (abundant plural) and 'amwah' (paucal plural). 'Al-'ayn' (spring) is a homonym between the sun, spring, gold, dinar, wealth, cash, spy, rain, wild cow offspring, best of something, the essence of something, a small group of people, a letter of the alphabet, what is to the right of the qiblah of Iraq, a skin lesion, and others. The intended meaning here is the spring (yambu') due to context. In his statement 'and the sea' conjuncted to 'the sky', i.e., 'and with seawater', is an indication of refuting those who say seawater is not water, to the extent that it is narrated from Ibn 'Imran that he said regarding seawater: 'Tayammum is more beloved to me than it,' as quoted from him in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. He divided these waters according to what is commonly observed, otherwise all are from the sky due to His statement: 'Do you not see that Allah sends down water from the sky and then makes it springs in the earth?' [al-Zumar: 21]. It was objected that the verse does not indicate that all waters descend from the sky because 'ma' is an indefinite noun in an affirmative statement, and it is known that it does not imply generality. We respond: Rather it does imply generality due to the context of bestowal of favor, for Allah mentioned it in the context of bestowing favor, so if it did not indicate generality, the purpose would be missed. The indefinite noun in affirmative statements indicates generality when a context indicates it, as in His statement: 'Every soul will know what it has brought' [al-Takwir: 14], meaning every soul. Know that water is of two types: absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad). The absolute is what comes to mind when we say 'water' absolutely, and no impurity nor any meaning preventing the validity of prayer has taken effect on it. Thus excluded is restricted water, impure water, and used water. 'Absolute' in principles refers to what pertains to the essence without attributes, neither by negation nor by? His statement: (And the intended meaning here is the first, i.e., permissibility) because purification consists of actions like wudu and the like. In Sharh al-Shaykh Isma'il: 'The apparent meaning here is validity, regardless of permissibility or lack thereof.' (And whoever says based on the general application of the homonym that 'jawaz' is used here in both senses) I say: As for the explanation of its use in the sense of permissibility, it is as previously mentioned. As for its use in the sense of validity, it is because validity is necessarily entailed by permissibility without vice versa, and this is the case here. For purification may be valid and permissible, and may be valid but not permissible, such as purification with permissible water or with water for someone other than? His statement: (And the intended meaning here is the spring, due to context) It is said in al-Nahr: 'This is based on it being coordinated with 'water' and after it, it is not hidden. The better view is to coordinate it with 'sky', in which case it is not a homonym among what was mentioned. Yes, it is a homonym between it and the water of the eye, and the second is not intended due to context.' End quote. And one can assume an elided phrase in the commentator's words, i.e., 'water of the spring', so it resolves to what was mentioned.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'ويتوضأ بماء السماء والعين والبحر' (And one performs wudu with rainwater, spring water, and seawater). The commentator, Imam Ibn Nujaym, begins by clarifying the term 'jawaz' (جواز) used in the ruling. He explains that 'jawaz' can mean either legal permissibility (halal) or validity (sihhah), and in the context of actions like wudu, the general usage is permissibility, though some scholars use it in both senses. He then defines water linguistically and etymologically, noting its root and plurals. Next, he discusses the word 'ayn' (عين), which is a homonym with many meanings, and clarifies that here it means 'spring' (yambu') due to context. The commentator also addresses a minority view that seawater is not suitable for wudu, refuting it by noting that the ruling explicitly includes seawater. He then discusses the origin of all waters, citing the Quranic verse that water descends from the sky and then becomes springs. An objection is raised that the verse does not imply all waters come from the sky, but the commentator refutes this by arguing that the context of divine favor indicates generality. He then introduces the classification of water into absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad), defining absolute water as that which is naturally understood as water and free from impurities or conditions that prevent prayer. Finally, he returns to the term 'jawaz' to reaffirm that in this context it means permissibility, but also notes the validity aspect. The passage is a thorough legal analysis of the types of water permissible for purification according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawaz): In this passage, 'jawaz' is used to mean either legal permissibility (halal) or validity (sihhah), with the commentator favoring permissibility for actions like wudu.
الماء المطلق (al-ma' al-mutlaq): Water that is naturally understood by the term 'water' and is free from impurity or any condition that prevents the validity of purification.
العين (al-ayn): A homonym; in this context it means 'spring' (yambu') as determined by the context of the ruling.

Definition of Mutlaq and Muqayyad Water
Definition of absolute and restricted water with consensus on rainwater

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-ma' al-mutlaq and al-ma' al-muqayyad, and explain why rainwater is mutlaq and why consensus confirms its use for purification.

Core Idea
Absolute water is pure by nature; restricted water loses that status when mixed.

In this passage we explore the foundational classification of water in Hanafi fiqh: mutlaq (absolute) and muqayyad (restricted). Understanding this distinction is essential for determining what water can be used for wudu and ghusl. The passage also establishes the consensus (ijma') that rainwater – a prime example of mutlaq water – is valid for purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Water is classified as mutlaq (absolute) or muqayyad (restricted). Mutlaq water is called simply 'water', while muqayyad water requires a qualifier.
These categories determine ritual purity.
2
Determine the property of mutlaq water
Mutlaq water includes rainwater, spring water, and seawater. It retains its original nature and is pure and purifying.
The phrase 'like rainwater' indicates it is the default.
3
Determine the property of muqayyad water
Muqayyad water is mixed with something else, so it cannot be called 'water' without a qualifier (e.g., rose water). It loses the ruling of mutlaq water.
This distinction affects its use for wudu.
4
Recognize the proof for mutlaq water's permissibility
The passage cites ijma' (consensus) and Quran (8:11) to confirm that rainwater – and by extension all mutlaq water – is valid for purification.
Ijma' gives certainty to the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإثبات كماء السماء والعين والبحر والإضافة فيه للتعريف بخلاف الماء المقيد فإن القيد لازم له لا يجوز إطلاق الماء عليه بدون القيد كماء الورد. وقد أجمعوا على جواز الطهارة بماء السماء واستدلوا له بقوله تعالى وينزل عليكم من السماء ماء ليطهركم به

And they have agreed (ijma') on the permissibility of purification with rainwater and adduced evidence from His statement: 'And He sends down upon you from the sky water to purify you with it' [al-Anfal: 11].

Commentary
This passage defines the two main categories of water: absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad). Absolute water includes rainwater, spring water, and seawater; these are called water without any additional qualifier because they are inherently pure and purifying. Restricted water, like rose water, is not simply called 'water' but must be specified (e.g., 'rose water') because it is mixed with something else. The passage then states that there is consensus (ijma') among scholars on the permissibility of performing wudu with rainwater, citing the Quranic verse where Allah says He sends down water to purify. This consensus is a strong proof for the Hanafi position that all natural waters are suitable for purification.

Key Terms
الماء المطلق (al-ma' al-mutlaq): Water that is called 'water' without any qualifier, considered pure and purifying by itself.
الماء المقيد (al-ma' al-muqayyad): Water that is mixed with something else, such that it cannot be called simply 'water' but must be qualified (e.g., rose water).

The Meaning of 'Tahur': Hanafi Linguistic Argument
Hanafi rebuttal to Shafi'i/Maliki claim on meaning of 'tahur'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that 'tahur' means pure and evaluate how the intensive form implies purification.

Core Idea
'Tahur' is intensive pure, not transitive purifying—but intensification demands purification in context.

The word 'tahur' in the Quran and hadith lies at the heart of a classic inter-madhhab dispute over the nature of water for purification. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools argue that 'tahur' means purifying (mutahhir), while the Hanafi school insists it means pure (tahir) but with an intensive sense that necessarily entails purification. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi linguistic argument, focusing on Arabic morphology and the principle that intensification of an intransitive root implies a causal effect in context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed term
Locate the word 'tahur' in the Quranic verse and hadith cited. Recognize that both sides agree the texts are authentic, but they differ on the meaning of this single word.
The verse is al-Furqan:48; the hadith is about seawater.
2
State the two opposing positions
Clearly articulate: Shafi'i/Maliki claim 'tahur' = mutahhir (purifying); Hanafi claim 'tahur' = tahir (pure, with intensification). Note the practical consequence: if 'tahur' means purifying, then water must actively purify; if it means pure, any pure water suffices for wudu.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic proof
Follow the argument: 'tahur' is fa'ul from intransitive 'tahara'. In Arabic, fa'ul from an intransitive verb remains intransitive (e.g., nawwam from nama). Therefore, 'tahur' cannot be transitive like 'mutahhir'. The form only indicates intensification.
Contrast with transitive fa'ul forms like qatul from qatala.
4
Understand the semantic inference
The Hanafi commentator then argues: intensification (mubalaghah) in 'tahur' over 'tahir' must indicate an additional quality. Since purity itself is identical for two pure things, the only way to intensify is through the effect of purifying others. Thus, 'tahur' does not mean 'mutahhir' literally, but its meaning implies purification.
This is a nuanced middle position: the word is not directly transitive, but its intensive sense necessitates purifying ability in the context of water.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafis rule that any water that is pure in itself is valid for wudu, even if it has been used (still pure but not actively purifying in the Shafi'i view). Shafi'is require water that is both pure and purifying.
This affects rulings on used water, seawater, and other waters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدل جماعة بقوله تعالى وأنزلنا من السماء ماء طهورا وبالحديث الصحيح الذي رواه مالك في الموطأ وأبو داود والترمذي والنسائي وغيرهم عن أبي هريرة قال : سأل سائل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : يا رسول الله إنا نركب البحر ونحمل معنا القليل من الماء فإن توضأنا به عطشنا أفنتوضأ بماء البحر؟ فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «وهو الطهور ماؤه الحل ميتته»(۱) . قال البخاري في غير صحيحه هو حديث صحيح. وقال الترمذي حديث حسن صحيح. وأورد أن التمسك بالآية والحديث لا يصح إلا إذا كان الطهور بمعنى المطهر مذهب الشافعي ومالك وأما إذا كان بمعنى الطاهر كما هو مذهبنا فلا يمكن الاستدلال كما هو والدليل على أنه بمعنى الطاهر قوله تعالى وسقاهم ربهم شراباً طهورا [الإنسان : ٢١] وصفه بأنه طهور وإن لم يكن هناك ما يتطهر به .. وقال جرير : عذاب الثنايا ريقهن طهور. ومعناه طاهر وأهل العربية على أن الطهور فَعُول من طهر وهو لازم، والفعل إذا لم يكن متعدياً لم يكن الفعول منه متعدياً كقولهم نؤم من نام وضحوك من ضحك، وإذا كان متعدياً فالفعول منه كذلك كقولهم قتول من قتل وضروب من ضرب. قلنا: إنما تفيد هذه الصيغة التطهير من طريق المعنى وهو أن هذه الصيغة للمبالغة فإن في الشكور والغفور من المبالغة ما ليس في الغافر والشاكر فلا بد أن يكون في الطهور معنى زائد ليس في الطاهر، ولا تكون تلك المبالغة في طهارة الماء إلا باعتبار التطهير لأن في نفس الطهارة كلتا الصفتين سواء فتكون صفة التطهير له بهذا الطريق لا أن الطهور بمعنى المطهر وإليه أشار في الكشاف والمغرب قال : وما حكي عن ثعلب أن الطهور ما كان طاهراً في نفسه مطهراً لغيره. قوله : (وبالحديث الصحيح الذي رواه مالك الخ لا يخفى أن الاستدلال مسوق على جواز الطهارة بماء السماء وما في الحديث ماء البحر» اللهم إلا أن يقال إنه مبني على ما تقدم من أن المياه السماء وسيأتي عنه جواب آخر. قوله : (كلتا الصفتين سواء الصفتان هما أصل الطهارة كلها من
And a group adduced evidence from His statement: 'And We send down from the sky pure water' [al-Furqan: 48] and from the authentic hadith narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta', Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, and others from Abu Hurayrah who said: A questioner asked the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, saying: 'O Messenger of Allah, we travel by sea and carry little water with us; if we use it for wudu, we will become thirsty. May we perform wudu with seawater?' The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'It (the sea) is pure (tahur) water, and its dead animals are lawful.' Al-Bukhari in other than his Sahih said it is an authentic hadith, and al-Tirmidhi said it is hasan sahih. It was objected that reliance on the verse and hadith is only valid if 'tahur' means purifying (mutahhir) – the school of al-Shafi'i and Malik – but if it means pure (tahir) as in our school, then evidence cannot be adduced as it is. The proof that it means pure is His statement: 'And their Lord will give them pure (tahur) drink' [al-Insan: 21], describing it as 'tahur' even though there is nothing to be purified with it. And Jarir said: 'The saliva of the white-toothed ones is pure (tahur).' It means pure. The Arabic linguists hold that 'tahur' is a form of intensification (fa'ul) from 'tahara' (to be pure), which is intransitive. If the verb is not transitive, its intensification form is also intransitive, like 'nawwam' from 'nama' (to sleep) and 'dahuk' from 'dahika' (to laugh). If it is transitive, then its intensification form is also transitive, like 'qatul' from 'qatala' (to kill) and 'darub' from 'daraba' (to strike). We reply: This form only indicates purification in a semantic way, namely that this form is for intensification. For in 'shakur' (grateful) and 'ghafur' (forgiving) there is intensification that is not present in 'ghafir' and 'shakir'. So necessarily there is an additional meaning in 'tahur' that is not in 'tahir'. That intensification in the purity of water can only be due to purification, because in purity itself both attributes are the same. Thus the attribute of purification is for it in this way, not that 'tahur' means 'purifying'. Al-Zamakhshari and al-Mughrib alluded to this. It is said: What is narrated from Tha'lab that 'tahur' is what is pure in itself and purifying for others. His statement: (And regarding the authentic hadith narrated by Malik, etc.) It is not hidden that the inference is directed toward the permissibility of purification with rainwater, and what is in the hadith is seawater, unless it is said that it is based on what preceded that waters are from the sky, and another answer will come. His statement: (Both attributes are equal) The two attributes are the basis of purity, all from...
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the meaning of the word 'tahur' (طهور) in the Quranic verse 'water pure' and the hadith about seawater. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools argue that 'tahur' means purifying (mutahhir), so the verse and hadith prove that water is purifying. The Hanafi school holds that 'tahur' means pure (tahir), not necessarily purifying. The objectors claim that if 'tahur' only means pure, then the verse and hadith do not prove that water purifies. The Hanafi response, detailed in the passage, uses linguistic arguments: the form 'fa'ul' for intensification, and if the root is intransitive, the intensive form is also intransitive (e.g., 'tahur' from 'tahara' meaning to be pure). However, the Hanafi commentator then argues that the intensive form does imply purification in a semantic way, because the intensification in purity must be due to the ability to purify others. This is a nuanced position: 'tahur' does not literally mean purifying, but its intensive form inherently suggests purification. The passage also notes that the hadith is used as evidence for the permissibility of seawater for wudu, and the commentator addresses a potential objection that the hadith is about seawater while the verse is about rainwater. The passage ends with a reference to the two attributes of purity.

Key Terms
طهور (tahur): An intensive form of 'tahir' (pure). The Hanafi school argues it means 'pure' but with an intensive sense that implies purification, while the Shafi'i/Maliki schools argue it means 'purifying' (mutahhir).
فعول (fa'ul): An Arabic intensive form used for adjectives, derived from verbs. Here it is applied to 'tahara' (to be pure) to form 'tahur'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, why does the intensive form 'tahur' imply purification even though it is derived from an intransitive verb?
A) Because the verb 'tahara' can be transitive in some dialects.
B) Because the intensive form always implies causing the action.
C) Because the intensification in purity can only be understood as the ability to purify others.
D) Because the hadith explicitly states that seawater is purifying.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, 'tahur' means pure with intensive force, implying purification by context, not by direct transitivity.

The Meaning of Tahūr and the Evidence for Purification with Water
Debate on whether tahur means purifier or pure, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the meaning of 'tahur' and explain the linguistic evidence and istihsan used to support it, as well as compare it to the Shafi'i/Maliki view.

Core Idea
The word 'tahur' in the Quran means 'pure', not 'purifier'—Hanafis use istihsan to permit purification with all water.

This lesson explores a fascinating linguistic debate between the Hanafi and Shafi'i/Maliki schools over the meaning of the word 'tahur' in the Quran and hadith. While both schools agree that purification with rainwater and seawater is permissible, they differ on how the primary texts prove this. The Hanafi school argues that 'tahur' means 'pure' (tahir) and relies on istihsan to permit its use for purification, whereas the Shafi'i and Maliki schools interpret it directly as 'purifier' (mutahhir). Understanding this dispute reveals the sophistication of Hanafi usul al-fiqh in balancing textual evidence and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the meaning of 'tahur'
Clearly articulate that the Hanafi school considers 'tahur' to mean 'pure' (tahir) in itself, not 'purifying' (mutahhir). Note that this is based on linguistic analysis and is supported by Quranic usage.
This is the foundational point of the dispute.
2
Identify the opposing school's view
Recognize that the Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that 'tahur' means 'purifier' (mutahhir). Understand the evidence they rely on, such as the hadith of seawater being described as 'tahur'.
Both schools accept the practical ruling; the difference is in interpretation.
3
Follow the Hanafi linguistic evidence
Examine the Hanafi proofs: Quran 76:21 describes a drink as 'tahur' without purification, poetry uses 'tahur' for saliva, and Arabic grammar shows that fa'ul from intransitive verbs (like tahira) does not indicate transitivity.
These demonstrate that 'tahur' can mean pure.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposition
Study the Hanafi argument that fa'ul indicates exaggeration, and in water, the only meaningful exaggeration is in its purifying quality. Also consider the alternative that 'tahur' can be a noun meaning 'that with which one purifies', making the evidence direct.
This shows how Hanafis reconcile the texts with their linguistic principle.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note that the practical difference is minimal: both schools permit purification with seawater and rainwater. The dispute is theoretical, affecting how the proofs are understood, not the ruling itself.
This highlights the importance of usul in interpreting texts.

Book of Purification. 123. With affirmation like water of sky, spring, and sea; the addition (al) is for definition, unlike restricted water where the restriction is necessary and it is not permissible to call it water without the restriction, like rose water. They have agreed on the permissibility of purification with rainwater and used as evidence His statement: 'And He sends down upon you from the sky water to purify you thereby' (8:11). And a group used as evidence His statement: 'And We sent down from the sky pure water' (25:48) and by the authentic hadith narrated by Malik in the Muwatta', Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi, Nasa'i and others from Abu Hurairah who said: A questioner asked the Messenger of Allah, saying: O Messenger of Allah, we travel by sea and carry little water; if we use it for wudu, we become thirsty; may we perform wudu with seawater? So the Messenger of Allah said: 'It is tahur, its water is pure and its dead animals are lawful.' Al-Bukhari said in other than his Sahih: it is an authentic hadith. At-Tirmidhi said: hasan sahih. It was objected that adhering to the verse and hadith is not valid unless tahur means mutahhir (purifier), the madhhab of Shafi'i and Malik. But if it means tahir (pure) as is our madhhab, then inference is not possible. The evidence that it means tahir is His statement: 'And their Lord will give them a drink of pure drink' (76:21), describing it as tahur even though there is nothing to purify with. And Jarir said: 'The sweetness of the young women, their saliva is tahur.' Its meaning is tahir. And the Arabic scholars agree that tahur is fa'ul from tahira which is intransitive; if the verb is not transitive, then fa'ul from it is not transitive, like nawum from nama and dahuk from dahika. If it is transitive, then fa'ul from it is likewise transitive, like qatul from qatala and darub from daraba. We say: This form only conveys purification in a figurative sense, because this form indicates exaggeration; for in shakur and ghafur there is exaggeration not present in ghafir and shakir. Therefore there must be an additional meaning in tahur not present in tahir, and that exaggeration in the purity of water can only be with regard to purification, because in the essence of purity both attributes are equal. Thus the attribute of purification is for it in this way, not that tahur means mutahhir. This is what was indicated in al-Kashshaf and al-Mughrib. And what was narrated from Tha'lab that tahur is that which is pure in itself and purifies other than it. The context said in al-Nahr: This is based on it being conjunct to 'water', and afterwards it is not hidden; it is better to join it to 'sky', and thus it is not shared between what was mentioned. Yes, it is shared between it and the water of the well, but the second is not intended due to context. End. And one can estimate an omitted noun in the commentator's speech, i.e., water of the spring, so it returns to what was mentioned. His saying: (And by the authentic hadith narrated by Malik etc.) it is not hidden that the inference is directed to the permissibility of purification with rainwater, but what is in the hadith is seawater, unless it is said that it is based on what preceded that waters are from the sky, and another answer will come from him. His saying: (Both attributes are equal) the two attributes are the essence of purity... (hadith documentation) ... 124. Book of Purification. If this is an increase of eloquence in its explanation of purification, it would be correct, and it is supported by His statement: 'And He sends down upon you from the sky water to purify you thereby' (8:11). Otherwise, fa'ul is not derived from taf'il, and comparing it to what is derived from transitive verbs like qatul and manu' is not correct. And tahur comes as an adjective: 'pure water'; and as a noun for that with which one purifies, like wudu as a noun for that with which one performs wudu; and as a verbal noun: 'I performed tahur well', and from it is the saying: 'There is no prayer without tahur' i.e., taharah. If it means that with which one purifies, the inference is valid, and it does not need to be made to mean mutahhir, which would necessitate making an intransitive verb transitive. Thus some commentators have determined. And there is discussion from several aspects: First, that Allah described the drink of the people of Paradise with the highest attribute, which is purification. Second, that Jarir intended to give them preference over other women, so he described their saliva as purifier by which one purifies, due to their perfection and the pleasantness of their saliva and its distinction over others, and it is not interpreted as tahir because there is no merit for them in that, for all women's saliva is tahir; rather every animal with pure flesh is like that, such as camels and cattle. Third, his statement 'that exaggeration in the purity of water can only be with regard to purification' may be denied: the exaggeration is in abundance and quality in itself, not in purification. And what is meant by 'water of the sky' is rainwater, dew, snow, and hail if it is falling. It is reported from Abu Yusuf: it is permissible even if it is not falling. And the correct view is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is cited as evidence for the permissibility of purification with snow and hail what is established in... and the exaggeration in it. His saying: (And there is discussion) i.e., regarding what some commentators determined of the objection and answer, and the discussion is from three aspects: the first two on the objection, the third on the answer. And it is not hidden to the thoughtful that the third point repels the first two, so the previous objection remains valid and the answer 'We say: this form only conveys purification...' does not benefit it due to the third issue. I say: it is not hidden from you the weakness of these three aspects. As for the first two, because you have known, and because the objector previously relied on the principles of the Arabic scholars, and what the commentator mentioned from the two aspects is a mere claim without evidence. And it has been established among scholars of the etiquette of debate that the claimant who does not provide evidence is only prevented metaphorically, i.e., by demanding evidence for the premise, and this is not the case, so it is not valid. As for the third, because it is established that what was mentioned in the question and answer, and which is in the hadith, the question is about the permissibility of wudu with seawater. If the intended meaning of tahur in the answer is 'abundant purity' without purification, it would not benefit anything, because the gist of the answer then would be that it is permissible to perform wudu with it because it is abundant purity, and abundance of purity has no bearing on the place of purification, because the two attributes are equal in it as has passed. Far be it from the one who has attained the highest eloquence to intend that. So it is known that the intended meaning is exaggeration with regard to purification. When that is determined, the verse is interpreted according to this meaning, and thereby the correctness of what the commentator first mentioned appears, of using the hadith to prove the permissibility of purification with rainwater, even though it is reported about seawater. So his mention of it is to prove that the intended meaning of tahur in the verse is what was mentioned, but it is distant and open to discussion, so the better view is what we presented earlier. His saying: (And it is cited as evidence for the permissibility of purification with snow and hail...) this evidence has room for discussion, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the meaning of the word 'tahur' in the Quran and hadith, and its implications for purification with various types of water. The Hanafi position is that 'tahur' means 'pure' (tahir) in itself, not necessarily 'purifying' (mutahhir). The Shafi'i and Maliki schools hold that it means 'purifier'. The passage begins by affirming the permissibility of purification with rainwater, citing Quran 8:11 and 25:48, and the famous hadith about seawater being 'tahur'. The objector (the opposing school) argues that these proofs only work if 'tahur' means 'mutahhir', because if it only means 'pure', then the verse about sending down 'pure water' does not directly indicate purification. The Hanafi responds by providing linguistic evidence: in the Quran (76:21), 'tahur' describes a drink in Paradise, which is not used for purification; similarly, in a line of poetry, 'tahur' is used for saliva. The Arabic form 'fa'ul' from intransitive verbs does not imply transitivity, so 'tahur' from 'tahira' (to be pure) means 'pure' not 'purifier'. However, the Hanafi concession is that this form (fa'ul) indicates exaggeration (mubalaghah), and in the context of water, the exaggeration can only be in its capacity to purify, because all water is pure, so the excess must be in its purifying quality. But they maintain this is a figurative derivation, not a literal meaning. The passage continues with a discussion by commentators, resolving that 'tahur' can also be used as a noun meaning 'that by which purification is performed' (similar to 'wudu' as the act of purification). This makes the hadith and verses valid evidence without needing to change the meaning. The passage also addresses purification with snow and hail, noting that Abu Yusuf allows it even if not falling, but the correct view is the majority opinion (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that it must be falling. The overall argument is that the Hanafi school justifies the use of all natural waters for purification, relying on istihsan and the general permissibility, while the linguistic debate ensures the correct interpretation of primary texts.

Key Terms
طهور (tahur): A word describing water; in Hanafi view it means pure in itself; in Shafi'i/Maliki view it means purifying.
ماء السماء (ma' al-sama'): Rainwater, including rain, dew, snow, and hail.
الطهارة (al-taharah): Purification, both physical and ritual.

According to the Hanafi school, what does the word 'tahur' primarily mean in the context of water?
A) Purifier (mutahhir)
B) Pure (tahir)
C) Blessed (mubarak)
D) Abundant (kathir)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'tahur' means pure, but uses istihsan to permit purification with all natural waters, while the Shafi'i school directly interprets it as purifier.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Definition and Ruling of Ṭahūr Water Including Rain, Snow, Hail
Defines ṭahūr linguistically and legally, discusses permissibility of using rain, snow, hail, and salt water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ṭahūr as a category of water, state the Hanafi ruling on using snow and hail for purification, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on the condition of falling (mutaqāṭir).

Core Idea
Ṭahūr water is pure and purifying; snow and hail are valid for purification if falling, per the mufta bihi.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the meaning of ṭahūr in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, clarifying that it means actively purifying, not merely pure. It then turns to practical rulings: water from the sky includes rain, dew, snow, and hail. On snow and hail, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether they must be falling. The correct position is that they must be falling, supported by a hadith from the Sahihayn. Finally, salt water that freezes in summer and melts in winter is excluded.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic meaning of ṭahūr
Identify the three usages of ṭahūr: adjective (pure), noun (that by which purification is performed), and verbal noun (the act of purifying). The commentator argues that in the legal sense it means actively purifying.
The Quranic verse 'water to purify you' supports the active meaning.
2
List the types of water from the sky
The passage specifies rain, dew, snow, and hail as valid 'sky water' (māʾ al-samāʾ). Each is considered absolute water (mutlaq) and fit for purification.
Salt water is excluded because it is not absolute water.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on snow and hail
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require snow/hail to be falling (mutaqāṭir). Abu Yusuf permits even if not falling. The correct view (mufta bihi) is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
The condition of falling is unique to snow/hail; for rain, falling is naturally assumed.
4
Identify the proof (dalīl)
The Prophet's supplication 'O Allah, wash away my sins with water and snow and hail' indicates that snow and hail are purifying substances. This hadith is from the two Sahihs, thus authentic.
The hadith is used as evidence that snow/hail can be used for purification.
5
Understand the exception of salt water
Salt water that freezes in summer and melts in winter is not permissible for purification because it is not absolute water (it is saline). This is an exception to the general permissibility of sky water.
The opposite behavior (freezing in summer) distinguishes it from regular water.

إن كان هذا زيادة بيان لبلاغته في الطهارة كان سديداً ويعضده قوله تعالى ﴿وينزل عليكم من السماء ماء ليطهركم به﴾ [الأنفال: ١١] وإلا فليس فعول من التفعيل في شيء وقياسه على ما هو مشتق من الأفعال المتعدية كقطوع ومنوع غير سديد. والطهور يجيء صفة نحو ماء طهوراً واسماً لما يتطهر به كالوضوء اسم لما يتوضأ به، ومصدراً نحو تطهرت طهوراً حسناً ومنه قوله لا صلاة إلا بطهور» أي طهارة فإذا كان بمعنى ما يتطهر به صح الاستدلال ولا يحتاج أن يجعل بمعنى المطهر حيث يلزم جعل اللازم متعدياً. كذا قرره بعض الشارحين وفيه بحث من وجوه الأول أن الله تعالى وصف شراب أهل الجنة بأعلى الصفات وهو التطهير والثاني أن جريراً قصد تفضيلهن على سائر النساء فوصف ريقهن بأنه مطهر يتطهر به لكمالهن وطيب ريقهن وامتيازه على غيره، ولا يحمل على طاهر لأنه لا مزية لهن في ذلك فإن كل النساء ريقهن طاهر بل كل حيوان طاهر اللحم كذلك كالإبل والبقر الثالث أن قوله ولا تكون تلك المبالغة في طهارة الماء إلا باعتبار التطهير قد يمنع أن المبالغة فيه باعتبار كثرته وجودته في نفسه لا باعتبار التطهير. والمراد بماء السماء ماء المطر والندى والثلج والبرد إذا كان متقاطراً. وعن أبي يوسف: يجوز وإن لم يكن متقاطراً والصحيح قولهما، وقد استدل على جواز الطهارة بماء الثلج والبرد بما ثبت في الصحيحين عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يسكت بين تكبيرة الإحرام والقراءة سكتة يقول فيها أشياء منها : اللهم اغسل خطاياي بالماء والثلج والبرد. وفي رواية بماء الثلج والبرد. ولا يجوز بماء الملح وهو يجمد في الصيف ويذوب في الشتاء عكس الماء.
If this is an increase in the clarity of his eloquence concerning purification, it is sound and supported by His statement, the Exalted: 'And He sends down upon you from the sky water to purify you thereby' [Al-Anfal 8:11]. Otherwise, the form 'faʿūl' has nothing to do with the verbal noun of tafʿīl, and its analogy to what is derived from transitive verbs like 'qaṭūʿ' and 'manaʿ' is unsound. Al-Ṭahūr occurs as an adjective, like 'water pure'; as a noun for that by which one purifies, like wuḍūʾ is a noun for that by which one performs wuḍūʾ; and as a verbal noun, like 'I performed purification well.' From it is his statement: 'No prayer except with purification', i.e., purity. If it is in the sense of that by which one purifies, the inference is sound, and there is no need to make it in the sense of purify, as that would require making an intransitive verb transitive. This is what some commentators have established. There is discussion in several aspects: first, that Allah described the drink of the people of Paradise with the highest attribute, which is purification; second, that Jarīr intended to praise them over other women, so he described their saliva as purifying, used for purification, because of their perfection and the goodness of their saliva and its distinction from others, and it is not taken as pure because there is no merit for them in that—indeed all women have pure saliva, and even every animal whose meat is pure like camels and cows; third, that his statement that such hyperbole in the purity of water is only from the perspective of purification may be prevented because the hyperbole is due to its abundance and quality in itself, not from purification. What is meant by sky water is rainwater, dew, snow, and hail if they are falling. From Abu Yusuf: it is permissible even if not falling, and the correct view is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). They have inferred the permissibility of purification using snow and hail based on what is established in the two Sahihs from Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, used to pause between the takbir of ihram and the recitation, a pause in which he would say things including: 'O Allah, wash away my sins with water and snow and hail.' In another narration: 'with water of snow and hail.' And it is not permissible with salt water, which freezes in summer and melts in winter, opposite to (ordinary) water.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentator's expansion on the meaning of 'tahur' (طهور) in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author first discusses the linguistic usage of 'tahur' as an adjective, noun, and verbal noun, and argues that it means 'that by which purification is effected' rather than simply 'pure' (tahir). This distinction is crucial because the Quranic verse 'water to purify you' uses the word 'tahur', implying active purifying capability. The commentator then addresses a criticism about the hyperbole in the word. After this linguistic analysis, he turns to practical rulings: water from the sky includes rain, dew, snow, and hail. Regarding snow and hail, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require them to be falling (mutqātir) for validity, while Abu Yusuf permits even if not falling. The correct position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The proof is the hadith from the two Sahihs where the Prophet (peace be upon him) asked Allah to wash his sins with water, snow, and hail, showing they are purifying. Finally, salt water (that freezes in summer and melts in winter) is not permissible for purification, as it is not absolute water.

Key Terms
طهور (ṭahūr): That by which purification is performed; purifying water (adjective or noun).
متقاطر (mutaqāṭir): Falling continuously, like rain or snow falling from the sky.
ماء السماء (māʾ al-samāʾ): Water from the sky: rain, dew, snow, hail.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It must be collected in a container
B) It must be falling from the sky (mutaqāṭir)
C) It must be melted first
D) It must be mixed with rainwater

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits purification with snow and hail only if they are falling (mufta bihi), based on the Prophet's supplication, and salt water that freezes in summer is not permissible.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Water Mixed with Pure Substances: The Hanafi Position
Inter-madhhab dispute on the permissibility of water mixed with pure substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water mixed with pure substances and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Hanafi evidence.

Core Idea
Water mixed with a pure substance remains valid for wudu if the mixture is subdued and still called 'water'.

This lesson explores a classic inter-madhhab dispute: Does water that has changed due to mixing with a pure substance (like saffron or lotus leaves) remain valid for ritual purification? The Hanafi school, differing from the Shafi'i, holds that it does as long as the mixed substance is subdued (maghlub) and the water is still commonly called 'water'. We will examine the evidence and reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: water mixed with any pure substance, even if it changes one of its attributes, remains valid for wudu as long as the substance is subdued.
The substance must be pure (tahir) and not dominate the water.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its basis
Al-Shafi'i differentiates: if the substance is unavoidable (like algae), it is permissible; if it is something like saffron that water can do without, it becomes restricted (muqayyad) and invalid.
The Shafi'i criterion is whether the water is renamed and the substance could be avoided.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal using linguistic custom
Hanafis argue that common usage still calls it 'water' even with slight changes, and the term 'saffron water' does not negate the absolute name. Examples from rivers and ponds show the word is used despite changes.
Custom ('urf) is key in oaths and agency but not in ritual purity where the absolute name applies.
4
Examine the Hanafi evidence from the Sunnah
Four hadith: washing the deceased pilgrim with water and sidr, washing the Prophet's daughter, his own ghusl from a bowl with dough traces, and his command to Qays ibn 'Asim. All involve mixing and change yet were used for purification.
The deeds of the Prophet are primary evidence; the mixing is inevitable and the change does not affect the ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practically, Hanafis allow wudu with water that has slight change from saffron, flour, lotus leaves, etc. Shafi'is would not allow it if the substance could be avoided. The Hanafi position is relied upon in the school.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view; it is supported by strong evidence and practical ease.
